It is believed during his stay at Pakpattan, Guru Nanak composed the first nine stanzas of the ‘Asa-Ki-Var’ a composition contained in the ‘Guru Granth Sahib’.

From Pakpattan, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled on foot to the city of Multan.

In tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Pakpattan to Multan.

Located on the banks of the river Chenab, the town of Multan in the state of Punjab dates back to antiquity. Prehistoric texts mention that the legendry tale of Prahlad and Hiranyakashipu took place in this town. In the year 326BC, when Alexander, the Greek King invaded Multan, it was under the rule of the Mali dynasty. Hiuen Tsiang, the seventh century Chinese traveller has also written about Multan’s prominence. In the 8th century, Islam was introduced to Multan through the traders and invaders from central Asia. During Guru Nanak’s visit, Multan was an important center for Muslim Faqirs of different traditions. Amardeep Singh: Multan is a city of Saints. It is close to a place where the five rivers of the Punjab merge with the mighty River Indus. It is probably for this reason that the Sufi Saints believe that in Multan, one can lose one’s ego, akin to the loss of the individual identity of the five rivers of the Punjab.

We visit the mausoleum of Sufi mystic, Sheikh Baha-ud-din Zakariya.

Sheikh Baha-ud-din Zakariya was a 12th century spiritual leader who established the Surhawardiya order of Baghdad in this region. It is believed that he was from the lineage of Prophet Muhammad.

During their journey through the south of Punjab, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana visited the shrines of three of four Sufi friends - Sheikh Baha-ud-din Zakariya, Sheikh Jalaluddin Bukhari and Sheikh Farid Ganj-e-Shakkar. These Sufi Saints were the pioneers of the 13th century Chishti and Suhrawardiya orders. Famously known as the ‘Char Yaars’ their shrines are important places of Islamic religious congregation, probably a reason why Guru Nanak visited them.

Amardeep Singh:

> aape raseeaa aap ras aape raavanahaar || (Guru Nanak)

> It is itself the producer, the ambrosia and the annihilator. (Guru Nanak)

This profound verse about the Omnipresence of God was recited by Guru Nanak at this mausoleum of Sheikh Baha-ud-din Zakariya. When Guru Nanak arrived in Multan, the then ‘Makhdum’ of this mausoleum warmly welcomed him and the two spent days in spiritual dialogues.

‘Makhdum’ is an Arabic word which means a teacher. In the sub-continent, it is a title bestowed upon the spiritual head of a Sufi shrine.

> Makhdum Bahavadi at the mausoleum of Sheikh Baha-ud-din Zakariya mentioned that he is aware that Guru Nanak saw no difference between a Hindu and a Muslim. However, he inquired if the supreme power resides in both Hindus and Muslims. In reply, Guru Nanak sang, aape raseeaa aap ras aape raavanahaar || aape hovai cholaRaa aape sej bhataar || ra(n)g rataa meraa saahib rav rahiaa bharapoor || (Sri Raag, Guru Nanak) It is itself the producer, the ambrosia and the annihilator. It is itself the bride (Consciousness) in the bridal dress (body). It is itself the bridegroom (producer) and the bed (creation). Imbued with love is my Supreme Omnipresent. It is permeating and pervading in all. (Sri Raag, Guru Nanak)

Guru Nanak says, indeed the One supreme power resides in all. It is the Creator who permeates and beholds one and all.

> Daata diyaan mehfilaan sajaaN vaale change ne || Sohne te hardam darood rehande parhde || Ameen mehboob oh taa marde ni marde || Hazoor naal pakiyaan lagaauN vaale change ne || Aakaa diyaan mehfilaan sajaaN vaale change ne ||

Blessed are those who adorn the congregation. They forever remain engrossed in the attributes of Divine. Those imbued with love for the Beloved, never die spiritually. Those who strike an honest alliance with the Master are worthy. Blessed are those who adorn the congregation.

The ancient Prahladpuri Temple made in reverence of Bhagat Prahlad is located adjacent to the shrine of Sheikh Baha-ud-din Zakariya. In present times, this site is in ruins because its caretakers, the Hindu community of Multan, migrated to newer lands after the religious divide during the partition of 1947.

Amardeep Singh: Bhagat Prahlad in ‘gurbani’ (spiritual verses in ‘Guru Granth Sahib’) is referred as a ‘Gurmukh’, the one whose face is towards righteousness. This ancient temple in Multan is a testimony to the ‘Puranic’ (ancient) tale of Bhagat Prahlad.

‘Bhagavat Puran’, the prehistoric scriptural literature has a compilation of moral stories. In it, one of the tales is of Bhagat Prahlad and his father King Hiranyakaship, whose name means, the beholder of materialism, representing ego. The name Prahlad means unwavering positivity, which represents the strength to defy negativity. King Hiranyakaship had gained a boon which protected him from being killed by any human or animal, during day or night, indoors or outdoors or by any weapon. This grace made Hiranyakaship arrogant and he demanded to be worshiped. Bhagat Prahlad, nurtured by the values of his mother, Kayadhu, declined his father’s demand. This infuriated King Hiranyakaship, who connived with his sister Holika, who had a boon to remain unscathed by fire. When Holika walked into a blazing pyre with Prahlad, she perished, while Prahlad remained unscathed. Thereafter, defying all parameters of the boon, Narasimha, a half- human, half-animal, killed King Hiranyakaship. To commemorate this triumph of righteousness over oppression, Hindus celebrate Holi, the festival of colours. Even though Guru Nanak and Bhagat Prahald lived centuries apart, in the ‘Janamsakhi’ art works, the two are depicted in dialogue. In my humble opinion, these artworks convey the philosophical alliance between Guru Nanak and Bhagat Prahlad.

> Amardeep Singh: Guru Nanak mentions the essence of Prahlad’s devotion in the verse, dhuramat haranaakhas dhuraachaaree || prabh naarain garab prahaaree || prahalaadh udhaare kirapaa dhaaree || (Raag Gauri, Guru Nanak)

> The evil-minded Hirnyakaship (ego) committed evil deeds. The spiritually enlightened annihilate their ego. Prahlad (righteousness) is emancipated by imbibing kindness. (Raag Gauri, Guru Nanak)

Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled further south from Multan on the River Chenab to visit Uch.

Tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Multan to Uch.

In the year 325 BC, King Alexander, the Greek ruler, founded a city called Alexandria, which is believed to be the erstwhile name of the city of Uch. After the conquest of this region by central Asian invaders in the 7th century, Uch attracted many Sufi saints, hence known as Uch Sharif, the exalted city.

Amardeep Singh: The town of Uch is in the southern-most tip of the Punjab. In the 12th century, it was known as Deogarh, which means a stronghold of God. Today, this town is famous for the historic shrines of Sufi saints.

In Uch, we visit the mausoleum of the 12th century Sufi saint, Syed Jalaluddin Bukhari. A native of Bukhara in Uzbekistan, he was an Islamic missionary who was known as Surkh Posh for adorning red attire. He was popular amongst people of all strata, gender and beliefs. It is said that one of his renowned female disciples was Lal Ded, who broke social biases of gender and religious discrimination. Lal Ded embraced the overlapping philosophies of Sufism, Islam, Shaivism and Buddhism. In the region of Kashmir, Lal Ded is considered a patron saint of both, Hindus and Muslims.

Amardeep Singh: In the 13th century, the famous Sufi saint, Jalaluddin Bukhari from Bukhara moved to Uch and settled in this town. This shrine in Uch Sharif is where he lies buried. When Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana visited Uch, the then ‘Makhdum’ of this shrine, by the name of Haji Sheikh Abdul Bukhari received them.

The musty room containing Syed Jalaluddin Bukhari’s coffin has captivating interiors. Multiple wooden pillars holding the flat roof are carved with intricate designs. Walls are decorated with ornate glazed tiles, and the ceiling with hand painted floral and geometric designs cast in lacquer. The room is filled with the graves of other saints and relatives of the Sufi saint, Jalaluddin Bukhari. It seems as if they all are still holding a congregation.

Many Sikh authors have reported that relics of Guru Nanak are present with the ‘Makhdums’ of Uch.

We visit the home of Syed Zamurd Hussain Hasnie Al Hussaini the present ‘Makhdum’, the caretaker of the shrine of Syed Jalaluddin Bukhari.

Syed Zamurd Hussain Hasnie Al Hussaini: I am a humble servant of ‘Surkhposh’, the king of kings, Syed Jalaluddin Bukhari. In the times of one of our ancestors, the caretaker of Syed Jalaluddin Bukhari’s shrine, honourable Guru Nanak visited here. Guru Nanak presented a book containing the compilation of his verses to him.

Syed Zamurd Hassan Zamrud Bukhari: Guru Nanak presented his footwear, bangles and this book to one of our ancestors. These possessions have been passed on through generations. This is the book. Baba Guru Nanak himself scribed it.

On a closer look at the scripture claimed as a gift by Guru Nanak to the ‘Makhdums’, we found it to be a printed version of the ‘Sukhmani Sahib’ verses of Guru Arjan Dev, the fifth Sikh Guru. The other relics referred by the caretakers were not presented.

Even though these relics are not of antiquity, they are a medium through which the lineage of the ‘Makhdums’ of Uch associate themselves with Guru Nanak.

> sachahu orai sabh ko upar sach aachaar || (Sri Raag, Guru Nanak)

> Truth is higher than everything. However, higher still is truthful living. (Sri Raag, Guru Nanak)

Only if true to oneself, can one be true to others. Guru Nanak reminds us that Truth is high but living a life of truthful conduct is supreme.

While Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana were being hosted by Makhdum Haji Abdul Sheikh Bukhari, Makhdum Bahavadi of Sheikh Baha-ud-din Zakariya’s shrine of Multan, also arrived in Uch. The two ‘Makhdums’ decided to visit Mecca for performing ‘Haj’. Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana accompanied them on their boat from Uch. Sailing through the Rivers Chenab and Indus, they reached Sukkur in Sindh.

In Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Uch to Sukkur in Sindh. Amardeep Singh: Sindh is in the crossroads of land and sea routes. It borders Punjab and Balochistan in Pakistan and Rajasthan and Gujarat in India. Historically, cultures, civilizations, faiths and philosophies converged in Sindh. It is probably for this reason that Guru Nanak also visited Sindh to interact with different cultures and faiths.

Famous for the Mohenjo-daro civilization, Sindh derives its name because of it’s close proximity to the Sindhu River, which is also known as the Indus.

For centuries, Hinduism and Buddhism were the integral belief systems of this region. Later, Sufism too blossomed here. Historically, natives of Sindh have been known for their syncretism.

Guru Nanak’s message of universal fellowship and monism became deeply embedded in the socio-religious beliefs of Sindh. The philosophy of Guru Nanak resonated with the people of this region, primarily of Hindu background, who came to be known as ‘Nanakpanthis’.

At Sukkur, we visit the island in the River Indus where exists a historic ‘Nanakpanthi’ temple, known as Sadhu Bela.

Amardeep Singh: Sind is a mystical land which has always fascinated me. It is here that the faiths start merging. Whether one is a Hindu, Sufi or a Sikh, they become one.

At Sadhu Bela Temple, inscriptions of Guru Nanak’s verses on its walls are a testimony of the impact of Guru Nanak’s philosophy in this region.

In the sanctum sanctorum of the temple, both ‘Bhagvat Gita’, the Hindu scripture, and the ‘Guru Granth Sahib’, the Sikh scripture are enshrined. The walls are adorned with pictures of Sikh Gurus and Hindu deities.

The temple caretaker warmly received us and read a few verses from the ‘Guru Granth Sahib’, the Sikh scripture.

> Priest: dhiaa miaa kirapaa naanak kau sun sun jas jeevaau || Satnam Sri Waheguru || Bolo Guru Nanak Sahib ki Jai ||

Kindness, grace and compassion are sought by Nanak. Hearing about Divine attributes, I live imbibing them. True are spiritual words and the revered teacher. Glory to respected Guru Nanak.

Sadhu Bela temple gives an insight into the rich ethos of the Sindhi community.

Amardeep Singh: In the memory of Guru Nanak’s travel through Sukkur in Sindh, the ‘Nanakpanthi Udasin Sadhus’ established this site of Sadhu Bela in the centre of the Indus River.

Bankhandi Sadhu, a ‘Nanakpanthi’ from Nepal, moved to Sukkur in the year 1823 to spread Guru Nanak’s message of monism. An invocation to Bankhandi Sadhu is inscribed on a marble slab along with a verse of Guru Nanak from the ‘Guru Granth Sahib’, the Sikh scripture.

Amardeep Singh:

> motee ta ma(n)dhar uoosareh ratanee ta hoh jaRaau || kasatoor ku(n)goo agar cha(n)dhan leep aavai chaau || mat dhekh bhoolaa veesarai teraa chit na aavai naau || (Guru Nanak)

> If I make a palace of pearls, inlaid with jewels. If I scent it with musk, saffron and sandalwood to make it enchanting. Don’t let this affluence make me go astray so that I forget contemplating on consciousness. (Guru Nanak)

Interestingly, we observed that the historical inscriptions have been tampered. ‘Guru Nanak’ which was originally engraved on this marble plaque is painted over as ‘Guru Sri Chand’, the name of Guru Nanak’s son who is associated with the ‘Udasin’ (ascetic) tradition. This overwriting is one of the many indicators of the socio-political forces which fragmented the diversity of the culture of ‘Nanakpanthis’, the bouquet of believers of Guru Nanak.

> Amardeep Singh: . ikOankaar || pauri || naanak jaachai ek naam man tan bhaava(n)dhaa ||

One Universal Creator Stanza Nanak seeks contemplation on singularity, which is pleasing to mind and body.

The Sadhu Bela Temple gives an insight to the erstwhile rich synergic Sindhi community. Unfortunately, the ‘Nanakpanthi’ culture of Sindh was fragmented in the partition of 1947 based on religion. Insecurities overpower rational thinking creating distrust and disharmony amongst communities.

> bhau much bhaaraa vaddaa tol || man mat haulee bole bol || (Raag Gauri Guareri, Guru Nanak)

> Fear is regressive and extremely heavy in weight. It entices the intellect to prattle insignificant words. (Raag Gauri Guareri, Guru Nanak)

Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana accompanied the Makhdums of Multan and Uch and travelled on foot from Sukkur to Shikarpur.

In tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Sukkur to Shikarpur.

At Shikarpur, we visit Dada Rewa Chand’s residence to experience his love for Guru Nanak.

Dada Rewa Chand:

> dda(n)ddaut ba(n)dhan anik baar sarab kalaa samarath || ddolan te raakhahu prabhoo naanak dhe kar hath || firat firat prabh aaiaa pariaa tau saranai || naanak kee prabh benatee apanee bhagatee lai || (Guru Arjan)

I bow in adoration countless times to the all-pervading Omnipresent. Save me from wavering, O Omnipresent Master. Nanak requests to be saved by its extended grace. Having wandered, I have submitted myself in your sanctuary. Nanak requests the Omnipresent to be attached to its service. (Guru Arjan)

The local people are Sindhi Nanakpanthis. They revere Guru Nanak. People of Balochistan who come here, those who are from our community, especially Hindus, they also believe in Guru Nanak. Whichever temple is there in this area, be it of any religion, the ‘Guru Granth Sahib’ (Sikh scripture) is definitely enshrined in them. Whatever message was given by Guru Nanak, it was about imparting love and staying humble.

> man maiaa man dhaiaa man pa(n)khee aakaas || tasakar sabadh nivaariaa || (Guru Nanak) Mind is illusionary. Mind is a wanderer. Mind is like a bird flying in the sky. Thieves (senses) which mislead the mind are controlled by spiritual wisdom. (Guru Nanak)

Mind wanders like a bird in the sky of illusion, desiring to accumulate worldly wealth.

<<Sindhi children reciting verses from ‘Guru Granth Sahib’ (Sikh scripture).>>

> Ham guru gur nath re || Nanak naam hamaara re || (Sain Aijaz Shah Rashdi)

I am the Guru, Guru of the ‘Naths’. Nanak is my name. (Sain Aijaz Shah Rashdi)

We visit the Shankar Anand Bharati Darbar which is one of the oldest sites established in the memory of Guru Nanak’s visit to Shikarpur.

> Guru bhi main hoon, chela bhi main hoon || Nanak bhi main hoon, Sachal bhi main hoon || Nath bhi main hoon, Aadam bhi main hoon || Shiv bhi main hoon || Eesa bhi main hoon || Moosa bhi main hoon || Mere se koi juda nahi hai || Naam aleda hai || Ham guru gur nath re, Nanak naam hamaara re || Nanak naam hamaara re, Har ghat dhaam hamaara re || (Sain Aijaz Shah Rashdi)

I am also the Guru. I am also the disciple. I am also Nanak. I am also Sachal. I am also a ‘Nath’ (ascetic). I am also Adam. I am also Shiv. I am also Christ. I am also Moses. No one is separate from me. Only the names are different. I am the Guru. Guru of the ‘Naths’ (ascetics). Nanak is my name. Nanak is my name. I reside in every heart. (Sain Aijaz Shah Rashdi)

Amardeep Singh: Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana spent a few days in Shikarpur. Their stay on the banks of a river is depicted in this painting at the Shankar Anand Bharati Temple in Shikarpur. This temple is a syncretic place of worship for the ‘Nanakpanthi’ communities of this region.

From Shikarpur, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana headed back towards River Indus. They boarded a boat and sailed southwards. The Makhdum of Uch and the Makhdum of Multan took the land route towards Mecca.

From Shikarpur, we make a detour from Guru Nanak’s footsteps to visit Sehwan Sharif to explore memories of Bhagat Sadhna, a Saint whose philosophy was aligned with that of Guru Nanak’s.

> Ho laal mori pat rakhyo bala jhoole laalan || Sindhri da sevan da Shahbaaz Qalandar || Dama dam mast Qalandar || Sakhi Shahbaaz Qalandar || Meri har dam andar ||

O red-robed Qalandar (free-spirited), preserve my honour. Of Sindh and Sehwan, you are the royal falcon of Qalandars (non-conformists). Every breath of mine is for the Qalandar (free-thinker). I have befriended the noble Qalandar (unconventional). In my every breath, they reside.

Bhagat Sadhna, also known as Sadhna Qasai, was a saint born in 1180 AD to a Muslim butcher family in Sehwan Sharif, a city in Sindh province of Pakistan.

Much after the time of Bhagat Sadhna’s birth, the city became famous for the Shrine of Lal Shahbaz Qalandar.

> Utho rindo || Utho rindo || Piyo jaam e Qalandar || Talue sahr hai shaam e Qalandar || Qalandar laal laj paa le jhoole laa ||

Awaken, O intoxicated. Drink the ambrosia of Qalandar (free-thinker). In dawn and dusk resides the Qalandar (free-spirited). O Qalandar (free-thinker), grace me with honour, O Jhuley Lal.

The Sufi ‘Malangs’, the carefree mystics, and ‘Qalandars’, the saints who associate themselves with boundary less existence, associate themselves with this shrine. Bhagat Sadhna was also of the same spiritual disposition.

It is said, Bhagat Sadhna was presented a ‘Saligram’, an aniconic stone representing the Hindu deity Vishnu by a Brahmin. In paying respect, Bhagat Sadhna would first worship the stone and then use it for weighing meat for his customers. This action angered the Hindu Brahmins and the Muslims rebuked him for worshiping an idol. The authorities viewed this as disrespect of religions and sentenced him to being bricked alive in a wall. It is said that the wall fell and Bhagat Sadhna survived.

In my humble opinion, the incident of the wall breaking is a metaphor for Bhagat Sadhna’s disassociation from dogmatic norms prescribed by religious authorities.

Bhagat Sadhna spent most of his adulthood in the city of Sirhind in East Punjab in India where he breathed his last.

Even though Bhagat Sadhna and Guru Nanak were born centuries apart yet their core philosophies intertwined. One of Bhagat Sadhna’s verse is inscribed in the ‘Guru Granth Sahib’, the Sikh scripture.

> tav gun kahaa jagat guraa jau karam na naasai || si(n)gh saran kat jaieeaai jau ja(n)buk graasai || mai naahee kachh hau nahee kichh aaeh na moraa || aausar lajaa raakh leh sadhanaa jan toraa || (Raag Bilawal, Bhagat Sadhna)

> What is your value, O world teacher? If you do not guide me, how to erase negative actions? Why seek safety from a lion who is to be eaten by a jackal? I am nothing, I have nothing, and nothing belongs to me. For this reason, protect my honor. Sadhna is your humble servant. (Raag Bilawal, Bhagat Sadhna)

From Shikarpur, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana sailed on the River Indus and visited Mirpur Khas.

In tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Shikarpur to Mirpur Khas.

Amardeep Singh: While travelling through Sindh, Guru Nanak & Bhai Mardana stopped at the city of Mirpur Khas. This gurdwara in the centre of the city was built in their memory.

This gurdwara was abandoned during the partition of the Indian subcontinent in the year 1947 when the followers of Guru Nanak had to migrate from Mirpur Khas. From Mirpur Khas, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana continued their journey by boat on the River Indus. Travelling through what was then called the River Kori, which merged into the Arabian Sea, they reached the port of Lakhpat.

From Mirpur Khas, we travel back to Punjab in Pakistan to cross the Pakistan-India border and resume trailing Guru Nanak’s footsteps from Lakhpat in the Indian state of Gujarat.

Amardeep Singh: Kot Lakhpat was once a prosperous town. A fertile agricultural area, important for trade and pilgrimage. It hosted people of diverse belief systems.

The fate of Lakhpat was decided by the 1819 Rann of Kachchh earthquake. The ground sank by 15 feet, and filled the creeks with saline sea water, turning the once fertile region into barren land. The River Indus and Kori changed their course away from the city of Lakhpat. Thus, the once prosperous port town lost its importance. After the partition of 1947, this region has been transformed into a high security border zone.

Amardeep Singh: When Guru Nanak travelled to Kot Lakhpat, this town was known as Basta Bandar. This shrine was built by the ‘Udasins’ (ascetic) and is now managed as a gurdwara.

Land records indicate that this site was built in the early 19th century. The native ruler, in reverence of Guru Nanak and the ‘Nanakpanthi’ followers, had allotted a nearby village named Koriyani to this site to generate revenue for its maintenance.

From Lakhpat, we make a detour to visit Koriyani village at a distance of around 20 kilometers.

At Koriyani, the villagers guided us to a dilapidated building next to a lake. They mentioned, this site was built by the ‘Udasins’ (ascetics) and was managed by the same management who had built the site in Lakhpat in the memory of Guru Nanak. This building, which is now in ruins, was abandoned in the 1960s.

Rana Sodha: I am Rana Ji Ramoji Sodha. I live in Koriyani. We Sodha people are Rajputs. We arrived here from Pakistan in 1971. When I arrived here, this building was still intact. Even the roof was intact. Even now, we believe in Guru Nanak’s teachings. All the spiritually inclined people in our community believed in Guru Nanak’s teachings. Anyone who has adopted the path of spirituality is well acquainted with Guru Nanak’s teachings.

In departing from this Indo-Pak border region of Lakhpat and Koriyani, I reflect, even though human beings share certain characteristic with other species, their uniqueness is in their cognitive ability and co-operative flexibility. Yet, they falter and create divisions.

Guru Nanak compares different states of human mind with the characteristics of other species, prompting us to emulate the positive virtues and discard the negatives.

> kete rukh birakh ham cheene kete pasoo upaae || kete naag kulee meh aae kete pa(n)kh uddaae || (Raag Gauri Cheti, Guru Nanak)

> Many a times, I have behaved like a tree and a plant. Many a times, I have conducted myself like an animal. Many a times, I have transformed as the movement of a cobra. Many a times, I have flown like a bird. (Raag Gauri Cheti, Guru Nanak)

From Lakhpat, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana walked along the coastline and reached Koteshvar.

In Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Lakhpat to Koteshvar.

In the Kachchh district of Gujarat, Koteshvar is located at the mouth of the Kori Creek and is famous for the ancient Koteshvar temple.

Historically, ‘Shaivites’, the followers of Lord Shiva would visit both, the Koteshvar and Hinglaj temples as part of their pilgrimage. During Guru Nanak’s visit the ‘Kanphatta Jogis’ (split eared ascetics), the followers of Guru Gorakhnath, were the primary care takers of this site. It is believed that Guru Nanak had dialogue with them at Koteshvar.

Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana boarded a boat at Koteshvar and travelled on the Arabian Sea to the port of Somiani.

We now travel from India to Pakistan and resume tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps from Somiani in Balochistan.

Somiani is a coastal town northwest of Karachi. It was once a prosperous natural port, a hub for trade with Central Asia.

Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled on land from Somiani, and passing through Chandrakup, they reached Hinglaj.

In Guru Nanak’s footsteps, travelling from Somiani to Hinglaj, we visit Chandrakup, the cluster of Hingol mud volcanoes.

The word ‘Chandra’ means Moon and ‘Kup’ means a mud basin. One of the mud volcanoes, which is around 300 feet high, is a site of pilgrimage for the Hindus who visit the Hinglaj Temple. These mud basins which are formed by volcanic activities are considered as physical representation of Lord Shiva’s anger.

From Chandrakup, we proceed further to the Hinglaj Temple.

Hinglaj is situated on the west bank of the Aghor River in Balochistan, close to one of the mountains of the Makran coastal range.

> Ik dam rasni, kare jwaala || Noe kot ko deve jawaani || Hinglaj par cham gai raani || Har jan ke hridaye me jaani || Bhagat janaa ke khari rahaani || ikOankaar ka bhajan kare ni ||

She, in the form of Hinglaj, ignited herself. Amongst the nine forms, she (Hinglaj) is the giver of life. She has ordained herself on the Hinglaj mountains. She resides in one and all. She is the protector of the devotees. She sings the praises of One Omnipresent.

The shrine of Hinglaj Temple is in a small natural cave in the hilly area of the Lyari Tehsil. Known as one of the 51 sacred ‘Shakti Peeths’, it is dedicated to Shiva’s consort Sati. It is believed Sati’s severed head fell at this place. In the Hindu tradition, Hinglaj is an important place of worship for ‘Shaktism’, feminine worship. A stone, which is worshiped as Hinglaj Mata, is known as ‘Nani Ka Mandir’ by Hindus and ‘Nani Ka Haj’ by Muslims.

> During Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana’s visit to Hinglaj, the ascetics of the ‘Nathpanthi’ sect, had a strong presence in this region. Looking at Guru Nanak’s unique attire, they were unable to discern if he was a ‘Haji’, a Muslim pilgrim on his way to perform ‘Haj’, the Islamic pilgrimage, or if he was a Hindu pilgrim visiting ‘Nani Ka Mandir’. When they inquired about his faith, Guru Nanak sang, sach varat sa(n)tokh teerath giaan dhiaan isanaan || dhiaa dhevataa khimaa japamaalee te maanas paradhaan || jugat dhotee surat chaukaa tilak karanee hoi || bhaau bhojan naanakaa viralaa ta koiee koi || (Raag Sarang, Guru Nanak)

> Make honesty as fasting and contentment as pilgrimage. Make wisdom and contemplation as ablution. Make kindness as deity and forgiveness as chanting beads. People with such attributes are par excellence. Make equipoise as the loincloth and intuitive awareness as the purified kitchen. Make good deeds as the ceremonial forehead mark. Savour conscious thoughts as food, says Nanak says. Rare are people with such intent. (Raag Sarang, Guru Nanak)

Guru Nanak discounts religious and ritualistic constructs as rules of conduct prescribed by society. He says, those who give precedence to universal love are beings of a true religion. They are beyond the realm of concepts such as pilgrimages, identities, fasting and garb.

Amardeep Singh: In this dry and barren region of Balochistan, along the River Aghor, in the hills of Hinglaj, there is a site made in the memory of Guru Nanak’s visit, which we are going to go and explore.

These barren Rocky Mountains are daunting, their magnificence challenged our spirits and the scorching heat tested our resilience. Unsure of our physical capabilities, with conviction we started the climb. The initial ascent was difficult, the latter part was exhausting and the final destination was gratifying. I guess this process of scaling the mountain is synonymous to surmounting our daily challenges.

> ddoo(n)gar dhekh ddaraavano peieeaRai ddareeaas || uoochau parabat gaakhaRo naa pauRee tit taas || gurmukh a(n)tar jaaniaa gur melee tareeaas || (Sri Raag, Guru Nanak)

> Seeing the dangerous mountains like the ways of the world, I am terrified. It is so difficult to scale these high mountains as there is no ladder for support. The spiritually enlightened seek wisdom within to cross the worldly challenges. (Sri Raag, Guru Nanak)

Amardeep Singh: Respected sir, remarkable is the spiritual teacher.

Prem Balmiki: Accolades to the Goddess.

Amardeep Singh: How are you?

Prem Balmiki: This is Hinglaj. Here many prominent saints have come. Guru Nanak also came here. A little ahead are Guru Gorakhnath’s memorial as foot impressions and a sacred hearth.

Amardeep Singh: The ‘Nanakpanthi’ and the Hindu community of Balochistan and the Sindh region have made a memorial with a foot impression at this site which represents the visit of Guru Nanak in the region of Hinglaj.

Close to this site, there is another memorial with impressions of the foot of Guru Gorakhnath made by people of faith.

The memorial in the form of Guru Nanak’s foot impression is an endorsement of love and reverence of the local Hindu community for Guru Nanak. For me, the foot impression is a reminder for us to walk on the path of Oneness for which Guru Nanak had travelled to such remote and harsh terrains.

British author, A.W.Hughes in his book entitled ‘The Country of Balochistan’, published in the year 1877, has mentioned the ‘Gitchki’ tribe. He has classified them as Sikhs, the believers of Guru Nanak who resided in the Hinglaj, Makran and Kalat regions. After the partition of 1947, there are no ‘Gitchki’ Sikhs residing in these regions.

Amardeep Singh: After having spent some time with the mystics at Hinglaj, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled back to Somiani and took the sea route to the Persian Gulf.

Water that emerges from the ocean, momentarily creates an identity as a wave but soon loses its singularity and merges with the ocean. This phenomenon reminds me of Guru Nanak’s verse which explains the fallacy of individuality which gets dissolved when ‘Atma’, the awareness, becomes one with ‘Paratama’, the wisdom.

> aatamaa paraatamaa eko karai || a(n)tar kee dhubidhaa a(n)tar marai || (Raag Dhanasri, Guru Nanak)

> When inner consciousness merges with the Cosmic Consciousness, duality residing within perishes within. (Raag Dhanasri, Guru Nanak)

#### Discussion Pointers

Episode 20: Rangeen Guldasta (Colourful Bouquet)

This discussion framework analyzes Guru Nanak's third Odyssey as presented in this episode, focusing on his interactions with Sufi saints, the Nanakpanthi traditions, and his memories of the Indus Region of Pakistan. Guru Nanak's critique of ritualism, his insights on truthfulness, the impact of fear, the nature of consciousness, and the effects of Oneness reveal how his experiential knowledge fostered lasting philosophical connections among diverse cultures. His wisdom offers timeless guidance that transcends religious boundaries. Guru Nanak's travels serve as both a physical and spiritual journey, resonating across different faiths and regions, even as historical events like the partition of 1947 fragmented some of these connections.

##### Historical Discussion Pointers

1. What is the timeline of Guru Nanak's third journey, and why is it essential to grasp this chronology to comprehend the profound influence of his travels? The episode highlights that after cherishing moments with their families in Nankana Sahib and Sultanpur Lodhi, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana commenced their third odyssey, believed to have begun in 1520 AD. This journey took them westward to engage with key sites of religious significance for the adherents of Islamic belief. How does this quest embody Guru Nanak's overarching vision and commitment to fostering interfaith dialogue?

2. How did Guru Nanak's interactions with Sufi mystics create a rich tapestry of spiritual insights that motivated him to collate devotional literature? During his visit to Pakpattan, Guru Nanak and Sheikh Ibrahim exchanged philosophical insights through the spiritual verses. With Sheikh Ibrahim's assistance, Guru Nanak collated more verses of Baba Farid. This intensive collation was later inscribed in the 'Guru Granth Sahib'. What does this collaborative effort reveal about Guru Nanak's approach to preserving spiritual wisdom across religious boundaries for the benefit of all?

3. How did the establishment of vibrant Sufi shrines in the diverse regions transform the religious landscape that were visited by Guru Nanak? The episode highlights that in the 8th century, Islam was introduced to Multan through traders and invaders from Central Asia. During Guru Nanak's visit to the region, Uch in Multan was a significant centre for Muslim Faqirs from various traditions. It became a hub for many Sufi saints, which is reflected in its name, Uch Sharif, meaning "the exalted city." How did these Sufi centres facilitate opportunities for spiritual dialogue?

4. In the regions visited by Guru Nanak, what influence did his travels have on the importance of the 'Nanakpanthi' tradition, and how was this tradition affected by historical events?

The episode states that Guru Nanak's message of universal fellowship and monism became deeply embedded in Sindh's socio-religious beliefs. Guru Nanak's philosophy resonated with the people of this region, primarily of Hindu background, who came to be known as 'Nanakpanthis'. How did the partition of 1947 impact this syncretic tradition, as evidenced in the references to abandoned sites and fragmented communities?

5. How did local communities preserve the memory of Guru Nanak's visits to different regions? Throughout the episode, we encounter references to sites established to commemorate Guru Nanak's visits. One notable example is the Shankar Anand Bharati Darbar, which is one of the oldest sites dedicated to honouring Guru Nanak's time in Shikarpur and his journey through Sukkur in Sindh. Additionally, the 'Nanakpanthi Udasin Sadhus' founded the Sadhu Bela site in the centre of the Indus River. What do these commemorative sites reveal about the lasting impact of Guru Nanak's visits and his significance in the region's spiritual heritage?

##### Philosophical Discussion Pointers

1. How does Guru Nanak's concept of truthful living transcend religious boundaries? In the episode, Guru Nanak expresses that Truth is higher than everything. However, higher still is truthful living. How does this perspective on truth as an action rather than merely a concept create bridges between different religious traditions he encountered during his travels?

2. What is Guru Nanak's critique of ritualism, and how does he reframe spiritual practice? When questioned by pilgrims about his faith at Hinglaj, Guru Nanak replied with his verse, which advised them to make honesty as fasting and contentment as a pilgrimage, wisdom and contemplation as an ablution, kindness as a deity, and forgiveness as the chanting beads. He further discounts religious and ritualistic constructs as rules of conduct prescribed by society. He says those who give precedence to universal love are beings of a true religion. How does this transform our understanding of spiritual practice?

3. In what ways does Guru Nanak illuminate our understanding of the mind and consciousness through his philosophical insights? The episode includes verses in which Guru Nanak expresses that the mind is illusory and wanders like a bird in the sky. He compares the senses to thieves who mislead the mind, suggesting the illusions of the senses can be comprehended through spiritual wisdom. In another verse, he explains that the duality within perishes when inner consciousness merges with Cosmic Consciousness. What does this reveal about his understanding of human psychology and spiritual transformation? 4. How does Guru Nanak's philosophy of Oneness reflect in his interactions with various faith traditions? When asked whether the supreme power resides in both Hindus and Muslims, Guru Nanak answered that this power is the creator, the source of sustenance, and the destroyer. The Supreme Omnipresent entity is filled with love and permeates everything. It embodies consciousness, just as a bride adorns her bridal attire. How does this perspective help dissolve religious boundaries?

5. How does fear contribute to the division among humans, as seen through the insights of Guru Nanak? The episode includes a verse from Guru Nanak expressing that fear is regressive and burdensome. It distracts the mind, leading to meaningless conversations. How does this insight help us understand the root causes of communal divisions, both in his time and ours?

---

<a id="episode-21"></a>
## Episode 21: Wahdat-al-Wajud — *Unity of Existence* ⭐

*Runtime 49:56 · [Watch on YouTube](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=LbzSbaxNSTw) · [Script PDF](https://thegurunanak.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/12/Episode-21.pdf)*

‘Wahdat-al-Wajud’, ‘Unity of Existence’, presents the resolute harmonizers conviction in wholeness. In the cradle of civilizations, Guru Nanak states that awareness of Omnipresence is an oasis that can quench the soul.

> mere saahibaa kaun jaanai gun tere || kahe na jaanee aaugan mere || (Raag Gauri Cheti, Guru Nanak)

> My Omnipresent master, who can know your glorious virtues? My demerits cannot be counted. (Raag Gauri Cheti, Guru Nanak)

One can feel Guru Nanak’s profound humility through expressions in his verses for he understates himself and glorifies the attributes of the Creator and its creation. On the contrary, I have a tendency of overrating my personality and noticing demerits in others.

From Somiani, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled to the ancient port of Al-Aswat, Mecca, Medina, Najaf, Karbala, Baghdad, Tabriz, Tehran and reached Mashhad. From Somiani, they sailed to Al-Aswat, and then proceeded to Mecca.

In tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Pakistan to Saudi Arabia.

Amardeep Singh: Saudi Arabia hosts the holiest Islamic cities of Mecca and Medina. With the birth of Prophet Mohammad, Islam emerged in these lands in the 7th century.

The modern day Kingdom of Saudi Arabia, founded in the year 1932, comprises of the two historical desert regions of Hejaz and Nejd. Saudi Arabia is the largest country in the Arabian Peninsula.

Amardeep Singh: Having travelled for days on rough seas, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana landed at the port of Al-Aswat in Saudi Arabia. From here they took the land route, crossing harsh and hot sand dunes and reached the city of Mecca.

The word ‘Mecca’ is believed to be a synonym for ‘Makkah’ which means, mother of all settlements. Located in a desert valley, this city was a commercial hub much before it became the holiest place of pilgrimage for the Muslims in the 7th century AD. The 1st century Greek writer Ptolemy refers to the city as Makoraba.

‘Kaabah’, the cube shaped structure in the center of the most sacred mosque in Mecca, is considered by Muslims as ‘Baitullah’, the house of God. During ancient times, before the advent of Islam, various tribes of the Arabian Peninsula would congregate to worship idols installed inside the ‘Kaabah’. These were removed by Prophet Muhammad, the messenger of Islam, who lived from 570 to 632 AD. ‘Hajar-ul-Aswad’, the ‘Black Stone’, is an ancient rock which was venerated since the pre-Islamic times. It was set on the eastern wall of the ‘Kaabah’ by Prophet Muhammad and is a holy Islamic relic.

Muslims aspire to perform ‘Haj’, a pilgrimage to Mecca once during their lifetime. As a part of Islamic ritual, pilgrims circumambulate the ‘Kaabah’ and pay respect to ‘Hajar-ul- Aswad’, the ‘Black Stone’.

The word ‘Haj’ originates from Semitic languages. In Hebrew, it is related to the word ‘Haqqa’ which corresponds to righteousness. In Arabic, it connotes an effort to go for an expedition. Thus ‘Haj’ represents the self- effort to embark on an internal spiritual journey.

One day, during their stay in Mecca, while Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana were asleep, they were awakened by Jiwan, a ‘Haji’, who rebuked them for sleeping with their feet pointing towards the direction of the ‘Kaabah’. The Arabic word ‘Qibla’ denotes the fixed direction of the ‘Kaabah’. Muslims consider pointing one’s feet towards ‘Qibla’ as disrespectful.

Guru Nanak with his rational disposition reminded Jiwan about the Islamic philosophy of ‘Al-Waasa’ which means that the Divine manifests in all directions.

Guru Nanak’s hagiographies, the ‘Janamsakhis’ and Bhai Gurdas’s ‘Vaar’ mention that when Jiwan moved Guru Nanak’s legs in anger, the ‘Kaabah’ followed the same direction. Interestingly, a narrative involving the ‘Kaabah’ moving is also associated with Hazrat Rabiʿa al-Basri, a female Muslim Sufi Saint. In my humble view, the narrative of the physical movement of the ‘Kaabah’ is a metaphor for Omnipresence.

> At Mecca, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana reunited with Makhdum Bahavadi of Multan and Makhdum Haji Abdul Sheikh Bukhari of Uch, who had parted at Shikarpur in Sindh and taken the land route to reach Mecca. While camping together with the Makhdums of Multan and Uch, some ‘Hajis’ asked Guru Nanak about his religious affiliations. In reply, Guru Nanak sang, kesar kusam miragamai haranaa sarab sareeree chaR(h)naa || cha(n)dhan bhagataa jot inehee sarabe paramal karanaa || ghia paT bhaa(n)ddaa kahai na koi || aaisaa bhagat varan meh hoi || terai naam nive rahe liv lai || naanak tin dhar bheekhiaa pai || (Raag Tilang, Guru Nanak)

> Saffron, flowers and musk oil can embellish the body. Akin to sandalwood, a devotee's qualities are such that they impart its fragrance onto everyone. As clarified butter or silk are not contaminated, similarly, a devotee remains untouched by caste, religion or status. They bow in contemplation and remain absorbed in self-reflection. Nanak says, he begs for charity at their door. (Raag Tilang, Guru Nanak)

Guru Nanak endorses the philosophy of love for humanity over religious associations.

> Qazi Rukn-ud-din, the arbiter of the ‘Sharia’, the Islamic court in Mecca, expressed that the Islamic path is the most superior and invited Guru Nanak to embrace Muslim protocols. In response, Guru Nanak sang, mihar maseet sidhak musalaa hak halaal kuraan || saram su(n)nat seel rojaa hoh musalamaan || karanee kaabaa sach peer kalamaa karam nivaaj || tasabee saa tis bhaavasee naanak rakhai laaj || (Raag Majh, Guru Nanak)

> Make mercy the mosque, faith a prayer-mat and honest living the ‘Quran’. Make modesty the circumcision and good conduct as a fast. These are the qualities of being a Muslim. Make good conduct the ‘Kaabah’, honesty the spiritual guide and actions of good deeds as prayer and chanting. Carry such a rosary which is pleasing to your consciousness. Nanak says, only then can the honour be preserved. (Raag Majh, Guru Nanak)

Guru Nanak expressed the importance of virtuous living over mere adherence to ritualism. Speaking metaphorically, he told the Qazi to revere compassion as a mosque, spread trust like spreading of the prayer mat, acquire righteous earnings as the teaching of the Quranic scripture, imbibe modesty as circumcision, savour pure intentions as fasting and let positive deeds be the ‘Kaabah’.

In the footsteps of Guru Nanak, having been exposed to diverse thoughts, I often ponder, concepts that are considered important at a time, were not significant in the past and will transform in the future. Guru Nanak says, in this cosmic arena, everything is subject to evolution.

> naa ko mulaa naa ko kaajee || naa ko sekh masaik haajee || rieeat raau na haumai dhuneeaa naa ko kahan kahaidhaa || naanak saach rate bisamaadhee bisam bhe gun gaidhaa || (Raag Maru, Guru Nanak)

> There was no priest. There was no judge. There was no scholar, spiritual guide, or pilgrim. There were no subjects or rulers, no worldly ego; no one spoke and boasted about themselves. Nanak says, those attuned to Truth, attain bliss. In delight, they resonate with the attributes of Divinity. (Raag Maru, Guru Nanak)

Travelling on the desert route, from Mecca, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana headed north to Medina.

Medina, also known as Al-Madinah, which means the luminous city in Arabic, is considered after Mecca as the second holiest site by Muslims. It is a desert oasis in western Saudi Arabia surrounded by the Hejaz Mountains and volcanic hills. Before the advent of Islam, it was known as Yathrib.

In the city center is the mosque Masjid Al-Nabawi, where Prophet Muhammad, the messenger of Islam is buried. For Muslims, it is an important place of pilgrimage.

Religious segregation is a social phenomenon. For ages, non-Muslims have not been allowed to visit the city of Mecca and the Masjid Al-Nabawi in Medina. Guru Nanak, the epitome of Oneness, was beyond the boundaries of religious mandates. As he did not proclaim alliance to any sect, he would consciously dress in a manner that allowed him to assimilate with natives. This facilitated better communication. Since the news of Guru Nanak’s visit to Mecca had travelled ahead, Bhai Mardana, who was a Muslim by faith, anticipated obstacles ahead. He visited the Masjid Al-Nabawi at Medina to gauge the situation. Thereafter, he took Guru Nanak with him to visit the site to interact with the congregations.

> dheval dhevatiaa kar laagaa aaisee keerat chaalee || (Raag Basant Hindol, Guru Nanak)

> The places of worship are subject to taxes; this is the new way of the world. (Raag Basant Hindol, Guru Nanak)

Guru Nanak, an advocate of Oneness, saw restrictions and penalties for access to religious sites as discrimination amongst humanity.

> During the course of our journey, our team comprising of people from diverse faiths encountered restrictions to access places of worship because of their respective beliefs. At Hindu sites, Muslim members of our team were not permitted, similar to non-Muslims not being permitted to visit Mecca and Medina. Unfortunately, society remains fragmented despite the high ideals of enlightened beings who inspire and practice the unity of humanity. I am reminded of Guru Nanak’s verse, gala(n)ee asee cha(n)geeaa aachaaree bureeaaeh || manahu kusudhaa kaaleeaa baahar chiTaveeaaeh || reesaa kareh tinaaReeaa jo seveh dhar khaReeaaeh || (Sri Raag, Guru Nanak)

> With words we are good but in actions we are negative. Within mind we remain impure but outwardly project purity. Yet, we equate ourselves with those who are truly divine. (Sri Raag, Guru Nanak)

From Medina, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana took the desert caravan route to Najaf. This route was developed for pilgrims by Begum Zubaida, wife of Caliph Harun Rashid.

We fly from Saudi Arabia to Iraq and resume tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps from Najaf.

The city of Najaf is located approximately 30 kilometers south of the ancient city of Babylon. This city was established in 791 AD by Abbasid Caliph Harun Rashid in memory of Prophet Muhammad’s son-in-law, Hazarat Ali, who is buried at the Imam Ali Mosque. This grand structure with a gold gilded dome is a center of pilgrimage for Muslims who consider it as the third holiest Islamic site.

Wadi-us-Salaam, meaning the valley of peace in Arabic, is the largest cemetery in the world. Members of the Shia community from across the world aspire to be buried in this cemetery which is close to the mausoleum of Imam Ali. It is believed, on the day of judgement, the ones buried here will be raised along with their spiritual leader.

I reflect that humans worry to secure a place in heaven after death and forget the importance of living a meaningful life.

> miTee musalamaan kee peRai piee kumi(h)aar || ghaR bhaa(n)dde iTaa keeaa jaladhee kare pukaar || jal jal rovai bapuRee jhaR jhaR paveh a(n)giaar || naanak jin karatai kaaran keeaa so jaanai karataar || (Raag Asa, Guru Nanak)

> Mud from the graves of the Muslims will also reach the potter’s wheel. It will also be moulded into pots and bricks to be baked in the kiln. Simmering in fire of ego, humans weep as the fiery coal of self-reflection fall upon them. Nanak says, the One who created the creation, is aware of its creation. (Raag Asa, Guru Nanak)

Whether cremated or buried, the body eventually becomes mud, which reaches the potter’s urn to create pots and bricks for the houses of people of any belief system. The human body discriminates only when alive.

From Najaf, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana proceeded to Karbala, which is at a distance of 75 kilometers.

In tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Najaf to Karbala.

Located on the west of the River Euphrates, Karbala is a city in central Iraq to the southwest of Baghdad.

Amardeep Singh: We are in the holy city of Karbala, the martyrdom and the resting place of Imam Husayn, the grandson of Prophet Mohammad. Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana, on their way to Baghdad, passed through the city of Karbala.

The famous battle of Karbala which took place in 680 AD is an important incident in Islamic history. Its outcome resulted in the relationship between the two most prominent sects of Islam; the Shiites and the Sunnis. Minute details of the battle of Karbala became the hallmark of the Shiite faith and culture. Pilgrims visit the tomb of Karbala to observe ‘Ashura’, which marks the martyrdom of Imam Husayn and his supporters during the battle of Karbala.

The sacrifice of Imam Husayn is a testament of his deeply held objective of establishing the foundations of a holistic character of justice and freedom over tyranny and subjugation. In reverence for the martyrs, devotees indulge in self-mutilation rituals, lamenting and cursing the slayers.

> rab kee rajai ma(n)ne sir upar karataa ma(n)ne aap gavaavai || tau naanak sarab jeeaa mihara(n)mat hoi ta musalamaan kahaavai || (Majh, Guru Nanak)

> Surrendering and respectfully obeying the will of nature, one gets rid of self-conceit. Nanak says, only when compassionate to all beings can one be called a Muslim. Raag (Majh, Guru Nanak)

A true homage to martyrdom is achieved by mutilating ego, lamenting for love and cursing disrespect for self and others. Guru Nanak says, the one who lives in the will of Almighty and is merciful is worthy of being a Muslim.

From Karbala, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled on the desert route and reached Baghdad.

In tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Karbala to Baghdad. Amardeep Singh: Iraq is historically known as a cradle of civilizations. Its capital, Baghdad, is nestled between the two rivers, Tigris and Euphrates.

Ibn Batuta, the 13th century traveller, mentions that the travel route from Najaf to Baghdad was a difficult path. Historic texts mention that Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana took the same path.

Bhai Gurdas writes in his ‘Vaar’ that on reaching Baghdad, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana rested at a place outside the city.

> fir baabaa giaa bagadhaadh no baahar jai keeaa asathaanaa || eik baabaa akaal roop dhoojaa rabaabee maradhaanaa || (Bhai Gurdas)

Then Baba went to Baghdad and stayed outside the city. One was Baba like the Divine and the other, Mardana, the ‘Rabab’ player. (Bhai Gurdas)

During Guru Nanak’s visit to Baghdad, the city was a part of the Persian Empire under the Iranian Shiite ruler, Shah Ismail of the Safavid dynasty. In Islamic history, this period is considered a turbulent one due to the increased conflicts between Shiite and Sunni Muslims as each sect had started asserting their political and religious authority.

> Reflecting on the divisions within faiths, I am reminded of Guru Nanak’s verse, musalamaan kahaavan musakal jaa hoi taa musalamaan kahaavai || aval aaul dheen kar miThaa masakal maanaa maal musaavai || hoi musalim dheen muhaanai maran jeevan kaa bharam chukaavai || rab kee rajai ma(n)ne sir upar karataa ma(n)ne aap gavaavai || tau naanak sarab jeeaa mihara(n)mat hoi ta musalamaan kahaavai || (Raag Majh, Guru Nanak)

> It is not easy to be called a Muslim. Only if earnest can one be called a Muslim. First, revere the spiritual teachings in humility. Scrape away ego and let the desire for material wealth be effaced. In becoming a true Muslim, deem righteousness as the spiritual leader and put aside the delusion of death and life. Surrender to the will of nature as reverence to the Creator and dispel self-conceit. Nanak says, only when compassionate to all beings, can one be called a Muslim. (Raag Majh, Guru Nanak)

Guru Nanak says associating oneself with a religious sect is easy. The real adherence is in imbibing the essence of the teachings of the faith. In Baghdad, we visit the seat of the Islamic Sufi ‘Qadri’ tradition.

Amardeep Singh: We are at the mausoleum of Pir Mohiuddin Abdul Qadir Gilani Sahib in Baghdad. He was the founder of the ‘Qadri’ sect. When Guru Nanak visited this city, then, Pir Abdul Rehman was the spiritual leader of this shrine. He was known as ‘Pir Dastgir’, the one who holds the hands for spiritual guidance.

One day, during their stay in Baghdad, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana, while in awe of divinity, were singing a verse which propounded the message of Omnipresence. They expressed that even though the ‘Vedas’, the Hindu scriptures, say that one can get weary of counting numerous worlds, ‘Katebs’, the Islamic scriptures, state that there are 18,000 worlds, but there is only One Omnipresent entity and rest is its manifestation.

> paataalaa paataal lakh aagaasaa aagaas || oRak oRak bhaal thake vedh kahan ik vaat || sahas aThaareh kahan katebaa asuloo ik dhaat || lekhaa hoi ta likheeaai lekhai hoi vinaas || naanak vaddaa aakheeaai aape jaanai aap || (Jap, Guru Nanak)

> There are nether regions beneath and hundreds of thousands of skies above. One can search and search for them until one grows weary; the ‘Vedas’ proclaim. There are 18,000 worlds as believed in the Islamic scriptures. However, the essence is One. If the numbers of the creation were countable, then one would perish scribing them. Nanak says, call that person great, the one who knows itself. (Jap, Guru Nanak)

Music was considered a taboo by some Islamic religious groups of this region. Perturbed with the blasphemous act, some people complained to Pir Abdul Rehman, the ‘Pir Dastgir’, who then personally visited Guru Nanak. The two engaged in spiritual dialogue after which ‘Pir Dastgir’ invited Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana to spend some time with the congregation at the shrine of Pir Mohiuddin Abdul Qadir Gilani.

At this shrine, Guru Nanak met Faqir Bahlool, the spiritual successor of Bahlool Dana, the 8th century spiritual Saint from Kufa. Faqir Bahlool invited Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana to stay with him at his humble abode in the outskirts of Baghdad city.

We now visit the mausoleum of Faqir Bahlool.

Amardeep Singh: We are at the site which was once the operational gurdwara in Baghdad, built in the memory of Guru Nanak’s visit as well as his meeting with Sheikh Bahlool. This site is close to the Tigris River and the Baghdad Railway Station. Punjabi soldiers of the British Indian Army, posted in Baghdad in the early 20th century, built a memorial adjacent to Faqir Bahlool’s mausoleum to commemorate Guru Nanak’s visit.

Due to the political unrest in Iraq, expats left the country and the gurdwara building was thereafter destroyed in the 2003 Iraq war. We meet Abu Yousuf, whose grandfather was a caretaker of the gurdwara when it was operational.

Abu Yousuf: My Grandfather used to serve at this gurdwara. After him, I continued to serve here. After the events of 2003 (Iraq War) some bomb shells fell on the site of Baba Nanak and also impacted the adjacent tomb of Bahlool-Al-Kufi. Greater damage was caused on the site of Baba Nanak. You can see the present condition of the site.

Amardeep Singh: This is the platform on which, I am told, the ‘Guru Granth Sahib’ used to be enshrined and behind us on this wall, there used to be a plaque, on which was described the history of this place.

Good times and challenging times are all transient. Experiencing Abu Yousuf’s warmth and his love for Guru Nanak, I have hope that Guru Nanak’s memories which have been erased due to the turbulent times will be re-established when peace returns in this region.

> aakhan vekhan bolan chalan jeevan maranaa dhaat || hukam saaj hukamai vich rakhai naanak sachaa aap || (Raag Majh, Guru Nanak)

> Talking, seeing, speaking, walking, living and dying, all these are transitory. Law of nature creates and nurtures. Nanak says, this itself is the Truth. (Raag Majh, Guru Nanak)

From Baghdad, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled north-east by land to Tabriz.

We travel from Iraq to Iran and resume tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps from Tabriz.

Iran is bordered to the northwest by Armenia and Azerbaijan, to the north by the Caspian Sea, to the northeast by Turkmenistan, to east by Afghanistan and to the southeast by Pakistan. In the 16th century, the commonly traversed route from Baghdad to Tehran was through the city of Tabriz in East Azerbaijan Province of northwestern Iran. The Moroccan explorer, Ibn-Batuta, who travelled to this region a century before Guru Nanak, wrote that Tabriz was then the capital town of the Mongols and was a commercial hub as it was located on the Silk Road.

Amardeep Singh: At the time when Guru Nanak travelled through this region, it was known as Persia. The people of this region at that time, as much as today, practice Islam. In Tabriz, we were unable to find any physical memories associated with Guru Nanak’s visit.

In the 19th century, a syncretic community of Guru Nanak’s adherents from Multan and Sindh in Pakistan had settled in this region as traders. They would hold spiritual congregations at the Ateshgah, the Fire Temple in the city of Baku in Azerbaijan, a site which was also revered by the Zoroastrians. On the outer walls of the Ateshgah Temple, Guru Nanak’s verses are inscribed along with Vedic Mantras. These inscriptions were commissioned by the ‘Udasin’ (ascetic) ‘Bawa Bankey Sahib’.

From Tabriz, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled to Tehran.

In tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Tabriz to Tehran.

On reaching Tehran, we visit the gurdwara, which was established in 1941 AD by the adherents of Guru Nanak.

Amardeep Singh: Iran once had a sizeable Sikh population in the cities of Tehran and Zahedan. A community that was sizeable, due to the recent political problems in the region has now reduced to a few hundreds.

The Iranians call this gurdwara, ‘Masjid-e-Hindan’, the mosque of the people of Hind. Reflecting on the amalgamation of the terms gurdwara and masjid, I am reminded a verse of Guru Nanak in which he says that there are different ways of religious calls from diverse places of worships but only those who listen and imbibe the divinity are the virtuous.

> baa(n)gaa buragoo si(n)n(g)eeaa naale milee kalaan || eik dhaate ik ma(n)gate naam teraa paravaan || naanak jin(h)ee sun kai ma(n)niaa hau tinaa viTahu kurabaan || (Raag Suhi, Guru Nanak)

> The prayer calls, playing of the flute and blowing of the horns call for well-being. Some are givers and some are beggars but only those who contemplate are acceptable. Nanak says, those who hear and imbibe the wisdom, I am sacrificed unto them. (Raag Suhi, Guru Nanak)

Daman Singh Bhasin: There is a lot of ‘Gurbani’ (verses) in ‘Guru Granth Sahib’ in the Persian language, which proves that he definitely did come to Iran. We have many ‘Shabads’ (verses). One of them is ‘Duniya Mukamey Fani’ (The world is a transitory place), which was recited by him and which shows that he was well versed with this area and the language as well.

Guru Nanak being intuitively multilingual, incorporated vernacular dialects of various regions to connect with the local people. In Iran, he sang a verse composed of Persian words.

> sab roj gasatam dhar havaa karadhem badhee khiaal || gaahe na nekee kaar karadham mam e(n)ee chinee ahavaal || badhabakhat ham ch bakheel gaafil benajar bebaak || naanak bugoyadh jan turaa tere chaakaraa(n) paa khaak || (Raag Tilang, Guru Nanak)

> Night and day, I wandered around in greed, contemplating evil schemes. I never did good deeds. Such is my condition. I am unfortunate, miserly, negligent, and shameless. Says Nanak, I am your humble servant. The dust at the feet of your slaves. (Raag Tilang, Guru Nanak)

In pursuit of finding happiness, humans indulge in greed and evil intentions. In humility, Guru Nanak says, he finds happiness in the feet of those who are inclined towards Divinity.

From Tehran, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled to Mashhad.

In tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel by train from Tehran to Mashhad.

Amardeep Singh: Ibn Batuta, Marco Polo, Nadir Shah and Alexander, all travelled through these lands, for the purpose of the expansion of their territories or to trade, and the world has heard many narratives about them. Yet, the world is unaware about the travels of Guru Nanak through these lands, while his mission was, essentially, to spread humanity and the Oneness of mankind.

Mashhad is a city located in the north-east of Iran. It is the capital of Razavi Khorasan Province and is a center of religious importance for Shiite Muslims. In reverence of the martyrdom of Imam Reza, the eighth Shia Imam, who died here in 818 AD, the city of Sanabad was renamed to Mashhad, meaning the place of martyrdom.

At Mashhad, we visit the shrine of Imam Reza.

According to ‘Bhai Mani Singh Janamsakhi’, in Mashhad, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana met Pir Abdul Rahman who was visiting the shrine on a pilgrimage from Gardez, a city in Afghanistan. Impressed by Guru Nanak’s ideology of attaching oneself to virtues as opposed to a religious sect, he was curious to know who was more blessed, the Sunnis who believe only in the Prophet Muhammad or the Shias who also have faith in Hazrat Ali.

Giving examples from the Islamic code of conduct, Guru Nanak replied, for him all are equal as the Divine light resides in everyone. Every soul plays a role in impacting his or her surroundings. The enlightened ones are guiding forces to show us how to pave our own chosen path to divinity.

> pa(n)j nivaajaa vakhat pa(n)j pa(n)jaa pa(n)je naau || pahilaa sach halaal dhui teejaa khair khudhai || chauthee neeat raas man pa(n)javee sifat sanai || karanee kalamaa aakh kai taa musalamaan sadhai || naanak jete kooRiaar kooRai kooRee pai || (Raag Majh, Guru Nanak)

> There are five Islamic prayers to be read five times a day and each has a name. However, let first be the attribute of honesty. Second, righteousness. Third, altruism. Fourth, the intention of goodwill for all. Fifth, imbibing Oneness. Repeat the prayer of good deeds and only then you may call yourself a Muslim. Nanak says, those who practice falseness only attain falsehood. (Raag Majh, Guru Nanak)

Guru Nanak’s message was that while the five daily prayers are adhered to by people according to the tenets of Islam, however, a devout Muslim who is true to the faith and truly reveres and honours its mentor, also imbibes the five essences; truthfulness, piety, unity, humility and contemplation.

Historically, Iran’s rich culture has been a source of inspiration and influence for many regions.

Dr. Bijan Reza Vala: I am an Iranian who has had the opportunity to spend many years in India and in those many years, I had the opportunity to come across the teachings of different faiths, including the Sikhism, which was the most impressive. The teachings of Guru Nanak Ji, who travelled so extensively at those times. The beauty of Guru Nanak Ji was, he used the Persian to beautify his teachings, which I really appreciate.

Reflecting on the present global politics which has isolated the country, I am reminded of Guru Nanak’s message that the human mind in its whirlpool of egoism, becomes incapable of distinguishing between the attributes which benefit or harm society.

> cha(n)gaa ma(n)dhaa kichh soojhai naahee ih tan evai khovai || (Raag Vadhans, Guru Nanak)

> Not distinguishing between positives and negatives, the essence of existence is wasted. (Raag Vadhans, Guru Nanak)

#### Discussion Pointers

Episode 21: Wahdat-al-Wajud (Unity of Existence)

The discussion points offer a framework for exploring the historical and philosophical dimensions of Guru Nanak's journeys through a predominantly Islamic region. By examining his interactions with religious authorities and his emphasis on unity amid sectarian tensions; we gain insight into the challenges he faced to create critical thinking amongst people. The philosophical questions highlight Guru Nanak's spiritual revolution, which prioritized ethical living over ritualistic practices and authenticity over outward piety, exemplifying his approach to interfaith engagement during a time of religious conflict. As mentioned in the episode, Guru Nanak's vision focused on spreading humanity and oneness, unlike others who travelled for territorial expansion or trade. By critically examining both the historical context and philosophical content of these travels, we can better appreciate the enduring relevance of Guru Nanak's message in today's divided world.

Historical Discussion Pointers:

1. What makes Guru Nanak's journey through the Islamic heartlands significant? The episode describes Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana's extensive travels from Somiani to Al-Aswat, Mecca, Medina, Najaf, Karbala, Baghdad, Tabriz, Tehran, and Mashhad. As noted in the text, during Guru Nanak's visit to Baghdad, the city was a part of the Persian Empire under the Iranian Shiite ruler, Shah Ismail of the Safavid dynasty. In Islamic history, this period is considered turbulent due to the increased conflicts between Shiite and Sunni Muslims, as each sect had started asserting its political and religious authority. How did Guru Nanak's travels provide opportunities for interfaith dialogue, fostering understanding and connection in a time of division and strife?

2. How did Guru Nanak navigate the religious restrictions and boundaries he encountered during his travels? The episode highlights an observation that religious segregation is a social phenomenon in which individuals of a specific faith are often excluded from visiting places of worship of other religions. However, it is noteworthy that Guru Nanak, the embodiment of Oneness, operated beyond the confines of religious mandates. He did not align himself with any specific sect and deliberately dressed in a way that allowed him to blend in with the local population. What does this strategic approach to cultural assimilation reveal about Guru Nanak's vision and determination to transcend religious boundaries to spread the message of Oneness?

3. What significant encounters did Guru Nanak have with Islamic scholars and spiritual leaders? Throughout his journey, Guru Nanak engaged with numerous religious authorities. He had a profound interaction with Qazi Rukn-ud-din, the arbiter of the 'Sharia', the Islamic court in Mecca. In Baghdad, he met Pir Abdul Rehman, the 'Pir Dastgir' and Faqir Bahlool, the spiritual successor of Bahlool Dana. How did these encounters demonstrate Guru Nanak's approach to interfaith dialogue, and what impact might these discussions have had on both Guru Nanak and his interlocutors?

4. Have the physical remnants of Guru Nanak's journey survived over time, or have they been lost? The episode explores the different outcomes of physical remnants associated with Guru Nanak's travels. In Baghdad, Punjabi soldiers from the British Indian Army, who were stationed there in the early 20th century, constructed a memorial next to Faqir Bahlool's mausoleum to commemorate Guru Nanak's visit. However, due to political unrest in Iraq, many expatriates left the country, resulting in the destruction of the gurdwara during the Iraq War in 2003. In contrast, a gurdwara established in Tehran in 1941 still stands today, despite the significant decline of the Sikh population in Tehran and Zahedan, which has dwindled to just a few hundred due to recent political challenges in the region. What does the preservation or destruction of these sites tell us about the challenges of maintaining interfaith heritage in regions of political instability?

##### Philosophical Discussion Pointers

1. How do the concepts of "Wahdat-al-Wajud" and Guru Nanak's vision of the unity of existence challenge the boundaries of traditional religions? The episode opens with the concept of 'Wahdat-al-Wajud (Unity of Existence)', which reflects the resolute belief in wholeness held by those who harmonize different perspectives. Throughout his journey, Guru Nanak consistently emphasized this philosophy of Oneness. When questioned about his religious affiliations in Mecca, Guru Nanak responded that a true devotee remains untouched by caste, religion, or status. How does this philosophy of unity directly challenge religious exclusivity, and what implications does it have for interfaith relations today?

2. Did Mecca shift its position as the narrative suggests during Guru Nanak's visit? The episode recounts a metaphoric narrative that during their stay in Mecca, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana were awakened by Jiwan, a Haji, who scolded them for sleeping with their feet pointing towards the Kaabah, as this is considered disrespectful. Guru Nanak responded by referencing the Islamic philosophy of 'Al- Waasa', which signifies that the Divine is present in all directions. When Jiwan moved Guru Nanak's legs in anger, the Kaabah miraculously followed the movement. This incident also parallels a similar story involving the Sufi saint Hazrat Rabiʿa al-Basri. This narrative raises an intriguing question: How does the concept of a moving Kaabah challenge our understanding of divine omnipresence? Furthermore, how does this idea influence our approach to religious practices?

3. What is the significance of Guru Nanak's emphasis on virtuous living over ritual practices? In response to Qazi Rukn-ud-din's invitation to embrace Islamic protocols, Guru Nanak sang a verse suggesting that mercy should be the mosque, faith the prayer mat, and honest living the Quran. He proposed that modesty be viewed as circumcision and good conduct as fasting. According to him, these are the essential qualities of being a Muslim. Similarly, regarding the five Islamic prayers, Guru Nanak recommended five spiritual qualities: first, the attribute of honesty; second, righteousness; third, altruism; fourth, the intention of goodwill for all; and fifth, the embrace of oneness. How does this emphasis on ethical living rather than ritual observance represent a revolutionary approach to spirituality, and what challenges might it have presented to established religious authorities and definitions of faith?

4. How do Guru Nanak's philosophy on humility and self-reflection contrast with religious orthodoxy? Throughout the episode, Guru Nanak emphasizes the importance of humility. In one verse, he expresses that only through true humility can one truly understand the glorious virtues of the Creator. He also critiques those who speak positively but act negatively; these individuals may appear pure on the outside, but their minds remain impure. They mistakenly consider themselves equal to those who are genuinely divine. How does this focus on authentic humility and self-reflection challenge the religious orthodoxy that often emphasizes outward displays of piety? What implications does this have for spiritual practice?

5. How did Guru Nanak convey the essential tenet of unity while respecting the diverse beliefs regarding the existence of various worlds? In Baghdad, Guru Nanak powerfully conveyed the concept of Omnipresence, a notion that breaks down religious barriers. He skilfully expressed the varied cosmological views from different traditions. The Vedas describe infinite worlds beyond our comprehension, while Abrahamic scriptures (Katebs) cite precisely 18,000 worlds. Yet, despite these diverse perspectives, Guru Nanak highlights a crucial truth that the essence of reality is fundamentally one, and everything else is just a manifestation of that singular essence. How does the concept of self-knowledge relate to understanding the Omnipresent entity described in Guru Nanak's philosophy? What might this relationship suggest about the purpose of philosophical and religious inquiry across different traditions?

6. How does Guru Nanak illuminate the fleeting nature of existence through his profound spiritual philosophy? Guru Nanak emphasizes the impermanence of material existence. The narrator at the Wadi-us-Salaam cemetery reflects on how humans worry about securing a place in heaven after death while neglecting the importance of living a meaningful life. Whether a person is cremated or buried, the body becomes dust. Guru Nanak expresses in his verses that talking, seeing, speaking, walking, living, and dying are all transient experiences. How does this perspective on impermanence inform Guru Nanak's approach to spirituality, and how might it offer a different perspective for understanding life's purpose compared to conventional religious adherence?

7. How does Guru Nanak's incorporation of multiple languages in his verses demonstrate his commitment to universal spirituality? This episode powerfully demonstrates how Guru Nanak adeptly embraced the vernacular dialects of various regions to engage with local communities. He exemplified extraordinary linguistic inclusivity by composing his verses in multiple languages. How does embracing diverse languages mirror Guru Nanak's philosophical commitment to transcending cultural and religious divisions? What does it indicate about his vision of using a compelling medium to convey spiritual wisdom at the grassroots level?

---

<a id="episode-22"></a>
## Episode 22: Lihaaze Insaniyat — *Respect for Humanity*

*Runtime 67:17 · [Watch on YouTube](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=fatBiRe_TTc) · [Script PDF](https://thegurunanak.com/wp-content/uploads/2025/12/Episode-22.pdf)*

‘Lihaaze Insaniyat’, ‘Respect for Humanity’, presents imprints of Guru Nanak’s spiritual legacy in ‘Khorasan’. At ‘Ghandhar’, the gracious humanist spreads the scent of humanity.

> aakhan sunanaa paun kee baanee ih man rataa maiaa || khasam kee nadhar dhileh pasi(n)dhe jinee kar ek dhiaaiaa || teeh kar rakhe pa(n)j kar saathee naau saitaan mat kaT jaiee || naanak aakhai raeh pai chalanaa maal dhan kit koo sa(n)jiaahee || (Sri Raag, Guru Nanak)

> Speaking and listening to wisdom without reflection, the mind remains coloured with materialism. The grace of Divinity is wholeheartedly bestowed upon those who imbibe wisdom and embrace Oneness. Thirty days of fasting may be observed, five daily prayers can be performed, but satanic thoughts can undo all such efforts. Says Nanak, walk on the honest path. Why remain engrossed in accumulating wealth and property? (Sri Raag, Guru Nanak)

Guru Nanak says, for the one engrossed in the materialistic realm, speaking and listening to words of wisdom to embrace Oneness remain transient like a gust of wind.

Disciplinary acts to control the mind are worthless if ‘Shaitan’, the internal satanic vices are not kept in check.

The oldest ‘Janamsakhis’ do not present a specific route taken by Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana from Iran to Afghanistan. Dr. Kirpal Singh in his book, ‘Janamsakhi Tradition – An Analytical Study’ is of the opinion that from Mashhad, Guru Nanak & Bhai Mardana travelled through Balkh and Mazar-i-Sharif in North Afghanistan to reach Kabul. The ‘Bhai Mani Singh Janamsakhi’ mentions that from Mashhad, they travelled through Kandahar in South Afghanistan. As there are narratives associated with Guru Nanak in Southern Afghanistan and not in the region of Balkh and Mazar-i-Sharif, hence we trace Guru Nanak’s footsteps through Kandahar.

From Mashhad, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana travelled through the cities of Kandahar, Ghazni, Kabul and Jalalabad. Thereafter, travelling through Kurram region, they crossed the Kurram Pass and reached Parachinar. They continued their journey through Ibrahimzai, Peshawar, Eminabad and reached Talwandi, which is now known as Nankana Sahib. From Mashhad, they first visited Kandahar.

Tracing Guru Nanak’s footsteps, we travel from Iran to Afghanistan and resume our journey from Kandahar.

O Divine companion, my friend is a Hindu, and I am a Muslim. O Divine companion, in love for my friendship, I sweep the ‘Dharamshal’, his place of worship. O Divine, my compassionate companion.

Kandahar, the second largest city of Afghanistan, is located on the banks of the Arghandab River, at an elevation of 3,310 feet.

Amardeep Singh: Kandahar derives its name from ‘Gandhar’. It is strategically located on the trade route that connects Middle-East and Central Asia with the Indian sub- continent.

‘Gandhar’ is a Sanskrit word meaning a land of aroma, probably referring to its population which comprises a mélange of cultures. Kandahar was founded by Alexander, the Greek King, and was later ruled by the Indo Gangetic rulers of the Mauryan Empire and the Buddhist Emperor Ashoka. The Central Asian and the Middle Eastern rulers introduced the Islamic culture to this region.

In Kandahar we visit the mausoleum of Sufi mystic Baba Wali Kandahari, whose association with Guru Nanak finds mention in the narrative at Panja Sahib Gurdwara in the city of Hassan Abdal in Pakistan.

The ‘Bhai Mani Singh Janamsakhi’ records that at Kandahar, Guru Nanak and Bhai Mardana were hosted by Baba Wali Kandahari. Some ‘Janamsakhi’ art that reflect this meeting in Kandahar depict Shah Sharaf seated with Guru Nanak and Baba Wali Kandahari. While it cannot be said with certainty, it is possible that the Shah Sharaf portrayed in the art was Sheikh Tatihar, the then spiritual head of Bu Ali Qalandar Shah Sharaf’s shrine, with whom Guru Nanak had earlier interacted during his visit to Panipat in his first odyssey.

According to an oral narrative, at the time of Guru Nanak’s visit, the governor of Kandahar was an unjust and arrogant administrator. He would visit the mosque five times a day to perform prayers. Unfair behaviour, however, can’t be compensated by performing obligatory religious rituals. Hence, Guru Nanak sang to convey a profound message.

> sau man hasatee ghiau guR khaavai pa(n)j sai dhaanaa khai || ddakai fookai kheh uddaavai saeh giaai pachhutai || a(n)dhee fook muiee dhevaanee || khasam miTee fir bhaanee || (Raag Malar, Guru Nanak) An elephant eats a hundred ‘maund’ (measuring unit) of clarified butter and molasses and five hundred ‘maund’ (measuring unit) of grain. It then belches, grunts and scatters dust. It regrets wasting precious breaths. In ignorance, blinded by self-conceit, the foolish perish. Only when immersed in self- reflection, they are delighted. (Raag Malar, Guru Nanak)

An elephant is focussed on consuming large quantities of food and it wiles away time in belching and scattering dust over itself. Guru Nanak analogizes that similarly a mind consumed with ego, belches away precious moments while being mired in self-conceit. He says, a delightful personality is attained through eradicating one’s ego.
